Argument	(ix), The doctrine of "prospective overruling" is a modern doctrine suitable for a fast moving society.
Argument	It does not do away with the doctrine of state decision but confines it to past transactions.
Argument	While in Strict theory it may be said that the doctrine 'involves the making of law, *hat the court really does is to declare the law but refuse to  give retroactivity to it.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is really a pragmatic solution reconciling the two conflicting doctrines, namely, that a court finds the law and that it does make law It finds law but restricts its operation to the future.
RatioOfTheDecision	It enables the court to bring about a smooth transition by correcting, its errors without disturbing the impact of those errors on past transactions.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the application of this doctrine the past may be preserved and the future protected.

Precedent	At the outset it would be convenient to place briefly the respective contentions under different heads : (1) The Constitution is intended to be permanent and, therefore, it cannot be amended in a way which would injure, maim or destroy its indestructible character.
Precedent	(2) The word "amendment" implies such an addition or change within the lines of the original instrument as will effect an improvement or better carry out the purpose for which it was framed and it cannot be so construed as to enable the <ORG> to destroy the permanent character of the Constitution.
Precedent	(3) The fundamental rights are a part of the basic structure of the Constitution and, therefore, the said pow er can be exercised only to preserve rather than destroy the essence of those rights.

Precedent	A correct appreciation of the scope and the place of funda- mental rights in our Constitution will give its the right perspective for solving the problem presented before us, Its scope cannot be appreciated unless we have a conspectus of the Constitution, its objects.

Precedent	528,563.
Precedent	788  It contains in a nutshell, its ideals and its aspirations.
RatioOfTheDecision	The preamble is not a platitude but the, mode of its realisation is worked out in detail in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution brings into existence different constitutional entities, namely, the <ORG>, the <ORG> and the <ORG> Territories.
RatioOfTheDecision	It creates three major instruments of power, namely, the <ORG>, the <ORG> and the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It demarcates their jurisdiction minutely and expects them to exercise their respective powers without overstepping their limits.
RatioOfTheDecision	They should function within the spheres allotted to them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some powers overlap and some are superseded during emergencies.
RatioOfTheDecision	The mode of resolution of conflicts and conditions for  supersession are also prescribed.
RatioOfTheDecision	In short, the scope of the power and the-manner of its exercise are regulated by law.
RatioOfTheDecision	No authority created under the Constitution is supreme; the Constitution is supreme; and all the authorities function under the supreme law of the land.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rule of law under the Constitution has a glorious content.
RatioOfTheDecision	It embodies the.
RatioOfTheDecision	modem concept of law evolved over the centuries.
RatioOfTheDecision	It empowers the <ORG> to make laws in respect of matters enumerated in the 3 Lists annexed to Schedule VII.
RatioOfTheDecision	In Part IV of the Constitution, the Directive Principles of State Policy are laid down.
RatioOfTheDecision	It enjoins it to bring about a social order in which justice, social.
RatioOfTheDecision	economic and political-shall inform all the institutions of national life.
RatioOfTheDecision	It directs it to work for an egalitarian society where there is no concentration of wealth, where there is plenty, where there is equal opportunity for all, to education, to work, to livelihood'.
RatioOfTheDecision	and where there is social justice.
RatioOfTheDecision	But, having regard to the past history of o ur country, it could not implicitly believe the representatives of the people, for uncontrolled and unrestricted power might lead to an authoritarian <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It, therefore, preserves the natural rights against the <ORG> encroachment and constitutes the higher judiciary of the <ORG> as the sentinel of the said rights and the balancing wheel between the rights, subject to social control.
RatioOfTheDecision	In short, the fundamental rights, subject to social control, have been incorporated in the rule of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	That is brought about by an interesting process.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the implementation of the Directive Principles, <ORG> or the <ORG> of a <ORG> makes laws in respect of matter or matters allotted to it.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the higher <ORG> tests their validity on certain objective criteria, namely, (i) whether the appropriate <ORG> has the legislative competency to make the law; (ii) whether the said law infringes any of the fundamental rights; (iii) even if it Infringement the freedoms under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 , whether the infringement only amo unts to "reasonable restriction" on such rights in "public interest."
RatioOfTheDecision	By this process of scrutiny, the court maintains the validity of only such laws as keep a just balance between freedoms and social control.
RatioOfTheDecision	The duty of reconciling fundamental rights in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 and the laws of social control is cast upon the courts and the touchstone or the standard is contained in the said two expressions.
RatioOfTheDecision	The standard is an elastic one; it varies with time, space and condition.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is reasonable under certain circumstances may not be so under different circumstances.
RatioOfTheDecision	The constitutional philosophy of law is reflected in Parts-1111 and IV of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The rule of law under the Constitution serves the needs of the people without unduly infringing their rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	It recognizes the social reality and tries to adjust itself to it from-time, to time avoiding the authoritarian pat@.
RatioOfTheDecision	EKery institution or political party that functions under the Constitution must accept it; otherwise it has no place under the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now, what are the fundamental rights ?
RatioOfTheDecision	They are embodied in Part III of the Constitution and they may be classified thus : (i) right to equality, (ii) right to freedom, (iii)right against exploitation, (iv) right to freedom of religion, (v) cultural and educational rights, (vi) right to property, and  (vii) right to constitutional remedies.
RatioOfTheDecision	They are the rights of the people preserved by our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	"Fundamental rights" are the modern name for what have been traditionally known as "natural rights".
RatioOfTheDecision	As one author puts: "they are moral rights which every human being everywhere at all times ought to have sim y because of the fact that in contradistinction with ot moral."
RatioOfTheDecision	They are the primordial ment of human personality.
RatioOfTheDecision	man to chalk out his own life in is rational and ry for the developrights which enable a he likes best.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our Constitution, in addition to the well-known fundamental rights, also included the rights of the minorities, untouchables and other backward communities, in such rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	After having  declared the fundamental rights, our Constitution says that all laws in force in the territory of India immediately before the commencement of the Constitution, insofar as they are inconsistent with the said rights, are, to the extent of such inconsistency, void.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution also enjoins the <ORG> not to make any law which takes away or abridges the said rights and declares such laws, to the extent of such inconsistency, to be void.
RatioOfTheDecision	As we have stated earlier, the only limitation c)n the freedom enshrined in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 of the Constitution is that imposed by a valid law rating as a reasonable restriction in the interests of the public.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will, therefore, be seen that fundamental rights are given transcendental position under our Constitution and are kept beyond the reach of <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time Parts 1111 and V constituted an integrated scheme forming a self-contained code.
RatioOfTheDecision	The scheme is made so elastic that all the Directive ' Principles of <ORG> Policy can reasonably be enforced 'without taking  - up.
RatioOfTheDecision	Cl/67-5 away or abridging the fundamental rights.

Precedent	A brief survey of the nature of the amending process adopted by various constitutions will bring out the futility of any attempt to draw inspiration from the said opinions or decisions on the said constitu tions.
Precedent	The nature of the amending power in different constitutions generally depends on the nature of the polity created by the constitution, namely, whether it is federal or unitary constitution or on the fact whether it is a written or an unwritten constitution or on the circumstances whether it is a rigid or a flexible constitution.
Precedent	Particularly the difference can be traced to the "spirit and genius of the nation in which a particular constitution has its birth".

RatioOfTheDecision	Broadly speaking amendments can be made by four methods (i) by ordinary legislative process with or without restrictions,  (ii) by the people through, referendum, (iii) by majority of all the units of a federal State; and (iv) by a special convention.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first method can be in four different ways, namely, (i) by the ordinary course of legislation by absolute majority or by special majority, (See Section 92 (1 )- of the British North America Act, sub-section 1 52 South African Apt, where under except sections 35 , 137 and 152 , other provisions could be amended by ordinary legislative process by absolute majority.
RatioOfTheDecision	Many constitutions provide for special majorities.
RatioOfTheDecision	); (ii) by a fixed quorum of members for the consideration of the proposed amendment and a special majority for its passage; (see the defunct Constitution of Rumania), (iii) by dissolution and general election on a particular issue; (see the Constitutions of Belgium, Holland, Denmark and Norway), and (iv) by a majority of two <ORG> in joint session as in the Constitution of the South Africa.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second method demands a popular vote, referendum , or plebiscite as in Switzerland, Australia, Ireland, Italy, France and Denmark.
RatioOfTheDecision	The third method is by an agreement in some form or other of either of the majority or of all the federating units as in Switzerland, Australia and the United States of America.
RatioOfTheDecision	The fourth method is generally by creation of a special body ad hoc for the purpose of constit ution revision as in Latin America.
RatioOfTheDecision	Lastly, some constitutions impose express limitation on the power to amend.
RatioOfTheDecision	(See Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	5 of the United <ORG>s Constitution and the Constitution of the Fourth French Republic).
RatioOfTheDecision	A more elaborate discussion of this topic may be found in the American political Constitution by <NAME>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will, therefore, be seen that the power to amend and the procedure to amend radically-differ from <ORG> to <ORG>; it is left to the constitution-makers to prescribe the scope of the power and the method of amendment having regard to the requirements of the particular <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no article in any of the constitutions referred to us similar to article 13(2) of our Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	India adopted a different system altogether: it empowered the <ORG> to amend the Constitution by the legislative process subject to fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Indian 1 Constitution has made the amending process comparatively flexible, but it is made subject to fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	800  Now let us consider the argument  that the power to amend is a sovereign power, that the said power is supreme to the legislative power, that it does not permit any implied limitations and that amendments made in exercise of that power involve political questions and that, therefore, they are outside judicial review, This wide proposition is sought to be supported on the basis of opinions of jurists and judicial decisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Long extracts have been read to us from the book "The Amending of the Federal Constitution (1942)" by <NAME>, and particular reference was made to the following passages :  "At the point it may be well to note that when the <ORG> is engaged in the amending process it is not legislating.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is exercising a peculiar power bestowed upon it by Article Five.
RatioOfTheDecision	This Article for the most part ,controls the process; and other provisions of the Constitution, such as those relating to the passage of legislation, having but little bearing."

RatioOfTheDecision	", he answers, "there is none".

RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his book "The Government of the United States", 5th Edition, uses strong words when he says "....... it is impossible to conceive of an unamendable constitution as anything but a contradiction in terms."
RatioOfTheDecision	The learned author says that such a constitution would constitute "government by the graveyards."
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his book "Constitutional Law of the United States" avers that the doctrine of amendability of the Constitution is grounded in the doctr ine of the sovereignty of the people and that it has no such implied limitations as that an amendment shall not contain a new grant of power or change the dual form of government or change the protection of the Bill of Rights, or make any other change in the Constitution.

RatioOfTheDecision	The learned author concludes that the amending clause is so fundamental to a constitution that he is tempted to call it the constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the learned author recognizes that difficulty in amendment certainly products circumstances and makes impossible the surreptitious abrogation of rights guaranteed in the constitution.

RatioOfTheDecision	He further implies limitations to the amending power from the belief in the constitution itself, such as, the republican form of Government cannot be abolished as it would be revolutionary in ifs characters.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the same book it is further said that the power to amend the constitution by legislative action does not confer the power to break it any more than it confers the power to legislate on any other subject contrary to the prohibitions.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his book "Modem Poliical Constitutions", 1963 edition, does not accept the theory of absolute sovereignty of th e amending power which does not brook any limitations, for he says.
RatioOfTheDecision	802  "In short, it attempts to arrange for the recreation of a constituent assembly whenever such matters are in future to be considered, even though that assembly be nothing more than the ordinary legislature acting under certain restrictions.
RatioOfTheDecision	At the same time, there may be some elements of the constitution which the constituent assembly wants to remain unalterable by the action of any authority whatsoever.
RatioOfTheDecision	These elements are to be distinguished from the rest, and generally come under the heading of fundamental law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus, for example, the American Constitution, the oldest of the existing Constitutions, asserts that by no process of amendment shall any <ORG>, without its own consent, be deprived of its equal suffrage in the <ORG>, , while among the Constitutions more recently promulgated, those of the Republics of; France and Italy, each containing a clause stating that the republican form of government cannot be the subject of an amending  proposals"  it is not necessary to multiply citations from text-books.

RatioOfTheDecision	One need not cavil at the description of an amending power as sovereign power, for it is sovereign only within the scope of the power conferred by a particular constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	All the authors also agree, that a particular constitution can expressly limit the power of amendment, both substantive and procedural.

RatioOfTheDecision	If some of the authors meant to say that-in our view, they did not-we cannot agree with them, for, in that event this <ORG> would no t be discharging its duty.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor can we appreciate the arguments repeated before us by learned counsel for the respondents that the amending process involves political questions which are, outside the scope of judicial review.
RatioOfTheDecision	When a matter comes before the <ORG>, its jurisdiction does not depend upon the nature of the question raised but on die, question whether the said matter is expressly or by necesssary implication excluded from its jurisdiction.
RatioOfTheDecision	Secondly, it is not possible to define what is a political question and what is not.
RatioOfTheDecision	The character of a question depends upon the circumstances and the nature of a political society.
RatioOfTheDecision	To put if differently, the court does not decide any political question at all in the ordinary sense of the.
RatioOfTheDecision	term, but only ascertains whether <ORG> is acting within the scope of the amending power.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be that <ORG> seeks to amend the Constitution for political reasons, but the <ORG> in denying that power will not be deciding on political questions, but will only be hol ding that <ORG> has no power to amend particular articles of the Constitution for any purpose whatsoever, be it political or otherwise.
RatioOfTheDecision	We, therefore, hold that there is nothing in the nature of the amending power which enables <ORG> to override all the express or implied limi- tations imposed on that power.

RatioOfTheDecision	There is an essential distinction between Constitution and statutes.

RatioOfTheDecision	There are two doctrines familiar to American Jurisprudence, one is described as Blackstonian theory and the other as "prospective over-ruling", which may have some relevance to the present enquiry.
RatioOfTheDecision	<NAME> in his Commentaries, 69 (15th edn., 1809) stated the common law rule that the duty of the <ORG> was "not to pronounce a new rule but to maintain and expound .the old one".
RatioOfTheDecision	It means the Judge does not make law but only discovers or finds the true law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The law has always been the same.
RatioOfTheDecision	If a subsequent decision changes the earlier one, the latter decision does not make law but only discovers the correct principle of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	The result of this view is that it is necessarily retrospective ,operation.

RatioOfTheDecision	Let us now consider some of the objections to this doctrine.
RatioOfTheDecision	The objections are: (1) the doctrine involved legislation by courts; (2) it would not encourage parties to prefer appeals as they would not get any benefit therefrom; (3) the declaration for the future would only be obiter, (4) it is not a desirable change; and (5) the doctrine of retroactivity serves as a brake on court which otherwise might be tempted to be so fascile in overruling.
RatioOfTheDecision	But in our view, these objections are not insurmountable.
RatioOfTheDecision	If a court can over-rule its earlier decision-there cannot be any dis- pute now that the court can do so there cannot be any valid reason why it should not restrict its ruling to the future and not to the past.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even if the party filing an appeal may not be benefited by it, in similar appeals which he may file after the change i n the law he will have the benefit.
RatioOfTheDecision	The decision cannot be obiter for what the court in effect does is to declare the law but on the basis of another doctrine restricts its scope.
RatioOfTheDecision	Stability in law does not mean that injustice shall be perpetuated.
Precedent	An illuminating article on the subject is found in <ORG>, [Vol.
Precedent	I 10 p. 650].
Precedent	813  It is a modem doctrine suitable for a fast moving society.
Precedent	It does not do away with the doctrine of stare decisis, but confines it to past transactions.
Precedent	It is true that in one sense the court only declares the law, either customary or statutory or personal law.
Precedent	While in strict theory it may be said that the doctrine involves making of law, what the court really does is to declare the law but refuses to give retroactivity to it.
Precedent	It is really a pragmatic solution reconciling the two conflicting doctrines, namely, that a court finds law and that it does make law.
Precedent	It finds law but restricts its operation to the future.
Precedent	It enables the court to bring about a smooth transi tion by correcting its errors without disturbing the impact of those errors on the past transactions.
Precedent	It is left to the discretion of the court to prescribe the limits of the retroactivity and thereby it enables it to would the relief to meet the ends of justice.
Precedent	In India there is no statutory prohibition against the court refusing to give retroactivity to the law declared by it.
Precedent	Indeed,.
Precedent	the doctrine of res judicata precludes any scope for retroactivity in respect of a subject-matter that has been finally decided between the parties.
Precedent	Further, Indian court by interpretation reject retroactivity.
Precedent	to statutory provisions though couched in general terms on the ground that they affect vested rights.
Precedent	The present case only attempts a further extension of the said rule against retroactivity.
Precedent	Our Constitution does not expressly or by necessary implica- tion speak against the doctrine of prospective over-ruling.
Precedent	Indeed, Arts.
Precedent	32, 141 and 142 are couched in such wide and elastic terms as to enable this Court to formul ate legal doctrines to meet the ends of justice.
Precedent	The only limitation thereon is reason, restraint and injustice.
Precedent	Under Art.
Precedent	32 , for the enforcement of the fundamental rights <ORG> has the power to issue suitable directions or orders or writs.
Precedent	Article 141 says that the law declared by <ORG> shall be binding on all courts; and Art.
Precedent	142 enables it in the exercise of its jurisdiction to pass such decree or make such order as is necessary for doing complete justice in any cause or matter pending before it.
Precedent	These articles are designedly made comprehensive to enable <ORG> to declare law and to give such directions or pass such orders as are necessary to do complete justice.
Precedent	The expression "declared" is wider than the words "found or made".
Precedent	To declare is to announce opinion.
Precedent	Indeed, the latter involves the process, while the former expresses result.
Precedent	Interpretation, ascertainment and evolution are parts of the process, while that interpreted, ascertained or evolved is declared as l aw.
Precedent	The law declared by <ORG> is the law of the and.
Precedent	If so, we do not see any acceptable reason why it, in declaring the law in superses-  814  sion of the law declared by it earlier, could not restrict the operation of the law as declared to future and save the transactions, whether statutory or otherwise that were effected on the basis of the earlier law.
Precedent	To deny this power to <ORG> on the basis of some outmoded theory that the <ORG> only finds law but does not make it is to make ineffective the powerful instrument of.
Precedent	justice placed in the hands of the highest judiciary of this country.
Precedent	As this <ORG> for the first time has been called upon to apply the doctrine evolved in a different country under different ,circumstances, we would like to move warily in the beginning.
Precedent	We would lay down the following propositions : (I) The doctrine of prospective over-ruling, can be invoked only in matters arising under our Constitution; (2 it an be applied only by the highest court of the country,  i.e., <ORG> as it has the constitutional jurisdiction to declare an binding on all the courts in India; (3) the scope of the retroactive operation of the law declared by <ORG> superseding its earlier decisions .is left to its discretion to be moulded in accordance with the justice of the cause or matter before it.
Precedent	We have arrived at two conclusions, namely, (1) <ORG> has no power to amend Part III of the Constitution so as to take away or abridge the fundamental rights; and (2) this is a fit case to invoke and apply the doctrine or prospective overruling.
Precedent	What then is the effect of our conclusion on the instant case ?.
Precedent	Having regard to the history of the amendments their impact on the social and economic affairs of our country and the chaotic situation that may be brought about by the sudden withdrawal at this stage of the amendments from the Constitution, we think that considerable judicial restraint is called for.
Precedent	We, therefore, declare that our decision will not affect the va lidity of the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964, or other amendments made to the Constitution taking away or abridging the fundamental rights.
Precedent	We further declare that in future <ORG> will have no power to amend Part III of the Constitution so as to take away or abridge the fundamental rights.
Precedent	In this case we do not propose to express our opinion on the question of the scope of the amendability of the provisions of the Constitution other than the fundamental rights, as it does not arise for consideration before us.
Precedent	Nor are we called upon to express out opinion on the question regarding the scope of the amends ability of Part Ill of the constitution otherwise than by taking away or abridging the fundamental rights.
Precedent	We will not also in- dicate our view one way or other whether any of the Acts questioned can be sustained under the provisions of the Constitution without the aid of Arts.
Precedent	31A, 31B and the 9th Schedule.
Precedent	815  The aforesaid discussion leads to the following results (1) The power of t he <ORG> to amend the Constitution is derived from Arts.
Precedent	245, 246 and 248 of the Constitution and not from Art.
Precedent	368 thereof which only deals with procedure.
Precedent	Amendment is a legislative process.
Precedent	(2) Amendment is 'law' within the meaning of Art.
Precedent	13 of the Constitution and, therefore, if it takes away or abridges the rights conferred by Part III thereof, it is void.
Precedent	(3) The Constitution (First Amendment) Act, 1951, Constitution (Fourth Amendment) Act, 1955, and,the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act, 1964, abridge the scope.
Precedent	of the fundamental rights.
Precedent	But, on the basis of earlier decisions of this <ORG>, they were valid.
RulingByPresentCourt	(4) On the application of the doctrine of 'prospective over-ruling', as explained by us earlier, our decision will have only prospective operation and, therefore, the said amendments will continue to be valid.
RulingByPresentCourt	(5) We declare that the <ORG> will have no power from the date of this decision to amend any of the provisions of Part III of the Constitution so as to take away or abri dge the fundamental rights enshrined therein.
Precedent	(6) As the Constitution (Seventeenth Amendment) Act holds the field, the validity of the two impugned Acts, namely, the Punjab Security of Land Tenures Act X of 1953, and the Mysore Land Reforms Act X of 1962, as amended by Act XIV of 1965, cannot be questioned on the ground that they offend Arts.
Precedent	13, 14 or 31 of the Constitution.
Precedent	Before we close, it would be necessary to advert to an argu- ment advanced on emotional plane.
Precedent	It was said that if the provisions of the Constitution could not be amended it would lead to revolution.
Precedent	We have not said that the provisions of the Constitution cannot be amended but what we have said is that they cannot be amended so as to take away or abridge the fundamental rights.
Precedent	Nor can we appreciate the argument that all the agrarian reforms which the <ORG> in power wants to effectuate cannot be brought about without amending the fundamental rights.
Precedent	It was exactly to prevent this attitude- and to project the rights of the that th e fundamental rights were inserted in the Constitution.
Precedent	If it is the duty of the <ORG> to enforce the directive principles, it is equally its duty to enforce them without infringing the fundamental rights.
Precedent	The Constitution-makers thought that it could be done and we also think that the directive prin-  816  ciples can reasonably be enforced within the self-regulatory machinery provided by Part III.
Precedent	Indeed both Parts III and IV of the Constitution form an integrated scheme and is elastic enough to respond to the changing needs of the society.
RatioOfTheDecision	The verdict of the <ORG> on the scope of the law of social control of fundamental rights is not final, but justiciable.
RatioOfTheDecision	If not so, the whole scheme of the Constitution will break.
RatioOfTheDecision	What we can- I not understand is how the enforcement of the provisions of the Constitution can bring about a revolution.
RatioOfTheDecision	History shows that revolutions are brought about not by the majorities but by the minorities and some time by military coups.
RatioOfTheDecision	The existence of an all comprehensive a mending power cannot prevent revolutions, if there is chaos in the country brought about by mis-rule or abuse of power.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand, such a restrictive power gives stability to the country and prevents it from passing under a totalitarian or dictatorial regime.
RatioOfTheDecision	We cannot obviously base our decision on such hypothetical or extraordinary situations which may be brought about with or without amendments.
RatioOfTheDecision	Indeed, a Constitution is only permanent and not eternal.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is nothing to choose between destruction by amendment or by revolution, the former is brought about by totalitarian rule, which cannot brook constitutional checks and the other by the discontentment brought about by mis-rule.
RatioOfTheDecision	If either happens, the constitution will be a scrap of paper.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such considerations are out of place in construing the provisions of the Constitution by a court of law.
RatioOfTheDecision	Nor are we impressed by the argument that if the, power of amendment is 'not all comprehensive there will be no way to change the structure of our Constitut ion or abridge the fundamental rights even if the whole country demands for such a change.
RatioOfTheDecision	Firstly, this visualizes an extremely unforeseeable and extravagant demand; but even if such a contingency arises the residuary power of the <ORG> may be relied upon to call for <ORG> for making a new Constitution or radically changing it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The recent Act providing for a poll in Goa, Daman and Diu is an instance of analogous exercise of such residuary power by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	We do not express our final opinion on this important question.
RatioOfTheDecision	A final appeal is made to us that we shall not take a different view as the decision in <NAME> case(1) held the field for many years.
RatioOfTheDecision	While ordinarily this <ORG> will be reluctant to reverse its previous decision, it is its duty in the constitutional field to correct itself as early as possible, for otherwise the future progress of the country and the happiness of the people will be at stake.
RatioOfTheDecision	As we are convinced that the decision in <NAME> case (1) is wrong, it is pre-eminently a typical case where this <ORG> should over-rule it.
RatioOfTheDecision	The longer it holds the field the greater will (1) [1952] S.C.R 89, 105 be the scope for erosion of fundamental rights.
RatioOfTheDecision	As it contains the seeds of destruction of the cherished rights of the people the sooner it is over-ruled the better for the country.
RatioOfTheDecision	This argument is answered by the remarks made by this <ORG> in the recent judgment in <ORG> v., <ORG>).
RatioOfTheDecision	"The third contention need not detain us ]'or it has been rejected by this <ORG> in <CITATION_608874> (2) .
Precedent	There a Bench of 7 Judges unanimously held that there was nothing in the Constitution that prevented the Supreme <ORG> from departing from a previous decision of its own if it was satisfied of its error and of its baneful effect on the general interest of the public.
Precedent	If the aforesaid rule of construction accepted by this <ORG> is in- consistent with the legal philosophy of our Constitu tion, it is our duty to correct ourselves and lay down the right rule.
Precedent	In constitutional matters which affect the evolution of our polity, we must more readily do so than in other branches of law, as perpetuation of a mistake will be harmful to public interests.
Precedent	While continuity and consistency are conducive to the smooth evolution of the rule of law, hesitancy to set right deviation will retard its growth.
Precedent	In this case, as we are satisfied that the said rule of construction is inconsistent with our republican polity and, if accepted, bristles with anomalies, we have no hesitation to reconsider our earlier decision."
Precedent	In the result the petitions are dismissed, but in the circumstances without costs.

RatioOfTheDecision	226 and 245on account of any provision in the Seventeenth Amendment and therefore no action under the proviso was necessary.
RatioOfTheDecision	it is only direct change in Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 and 245 which would require follow ing the procedure as to ratification or at any rate such change in other Articles which would have the effect of directly compelling change in Arts 226 and 245 and that in the present case no such direct compulsion arises.
RatioOfTheDecision	Lastly as to the argument of fear it is urged that there is always a provision with respect to amendment in written federal Constitutions.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a provision may be rigid or flexible.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our Constitution Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 provides for a comparatively flexible provision for amendment and there is' no reason to make it rigid by implying any limitations on that power.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further there Is no reason to suppose that all those things will be done by <ORG> which are being urged to deny the power under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 which flows naturally from its terms.
RatioOfTheDecision	Besides the above, reliance is also placed on behalf of the Union of India and the States on the doctrine.
RatioOfTheDecision	of stare decisis.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged that since the decision of this <ORG> in <NAME> case(1), sixteen further amendments have been made by <ORG> on  the faith of that decision involving over 200 Articles of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The amendments relating to Part III have been mainly with respect to agrarian reforms resulting in transfers of title of millions of acres of land in favour of millions of people.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore', even though <NAME> case(1) has stood only for fifteen years there has been a vast agrarian revolution effected on the faith of that decision and this <ORG> should not now go back on what was decided in that case.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further, besides the argument based on state decisis, it is urged on the basis of certain decisions of this <ORG> that the unanimous decision in <NAME> case(1) which had stood practically unchallenged for about'15'years till the decision in <NAME> case(2), should not be over-ruled unless it is found to be incorrect by a large majority of the Judges constituting <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged that if the present Bench is more or less evenly divided it should not over-rule the unanimous decision in' <NAME> sad's case(1) by a Majority of one.
Precedent	(1) (1952] S.C.R.
Precedent	89.
Precedent	(2) [1965] 1 S.C.R.
Precedent	933 We shall first take Art, 368.
Precedent	It is found in Part XX of the Constitution which is headed.
Precedent	Amendment of the Constitution" and is the only Article in that Part.
RatioOfTheDecision	That Part thus provides specifically for the amendment of the Constitution, and the first question that arises is whether it provides power for the amendment of the Constitution as well as the procedure for doing so.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not disputed that the procedure for amendment of the Constitution is to be found in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , but what is in dispute is whether Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 confers power also in that behalf.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now the procedure for the amendment of the Constitution is this: The amendment is initiated by the introduction of a Bill in either <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Bill has to be passed in each <ORG> by a majority of the total membership of that <ORG> and by a Majority of not less two-thirds of the members of that <ORG> present and voting.
RatioOfTheDecision	After it is so passed, it has to be presented to th e President for his assent.
RatioOfTheDecision	On such presentation if the President assents to the Bill, Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	3-68 provides that the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.

RatioOfTheDecision	368 , and those matters can only be amended if the Bill passed by the two <ORG> by necessary majority is ratified by the legislatures of not less than one-half of the States by resolutions to that effect.

RatioOfTheDecision	necessary ratification has been made, the Bill with respect to these matters is then presented to the President and on his assent being given, the Constitution stands amended.

RatioOfTheDecision	The argument is that there is no express provision in terms in Art.

Beside s it is a historical fact to which we can refer that originally the intention was to vest residuary power in States, and if that intention had been eventually carried out, it would have been impossible for any one to argue that the power to amend the Constitution was to be found in the residuary power if it had been vested in the States and not in the Union.

RatioOfTheDecision	There are a number of Articles in the Constitution, which expressly provide for amendment by law, as,.
Precedent	for example, 3, 4, 10, 59(3), 65(3), 73(2), 97, 98(3), 106, 120(2), 135, 137, 142(1), 146(2), 148(3), 149, 169, 171(2), 196, 187(3), 189(3), 194(3), 195, 210(2), 221(2).

Precedent	We,may in this connection refer to the following passage in The Law.and the Constitution by <NAME> (1933 Ed.)
Precedent	at p. 51 onwards :-  "A written constitution is thus the fundamental law of a cou ntry, the express embodiment of the doctrine of the region of law.
Precedent	All public uthorities-legislative, administrative and judical-take their powers directly or indirectly from it.....Whatever the nature of the written constitution it is clear that there "is a fundamental distinction between constitutional law and the rest of the law........

Precedent	It is because of this difference between the.
Precedent	fundamental law (namely, the Constitution) and the law passed under the legislative provisions of the Constitution that it is not possible in the absence of an express provision to that effect in the fundamental law to ,change the fundamental law by ordinary legislation passed thereunder, for such ordinary legislation must always conform to the fundamental law (i.e.
Precedent	the Constitution).

Precedent	245 and Art.
Precedent	248 read with item 97 of List 1, for that wou ld make any amendment of the Constitution impossible except with respect to the express-provisions contained in certain Articles thereof for amendment by law-.
Precedent	We may in this connection add that all this argument that power to amend the Constitution is to be found in Art.
Precedent	245 and Art.
Precedent	248 read with item 97 of List I has been based on one accidental circumstance, and that accidental circumstance is that the procedure for amendment of the Constitution contained in Art.
Precedent	368 is more or less assimilated to the procedure for making ordinary laws under the Constitution.
Precedent	The argument is that constitutional amendment is also passed by the two <ORG>, and is assented to by the President like ordinary legislation, with this difference that a special majority is required for certain purposes and a special majority plus ratification is required for certain other purposes.
RatioOfTheDecision	It may be admitted that the procedure for amendment under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is somewhat similar to the procedure for passing ordinary legislation u nder the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even so, as pointed out by Sir <NAME> in the passage already quoted, there is a clear separation between constitutional law and the rest of the law and that must never be forgotten.
RatioOfTheDecision	An amendment to the Constitution is a constitutional law and as observed in <NAME> case(1) is in exercise of constituent power; passing of ordinary law is in exercise of ordinary legislative power and is clearly different from the power to amend the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	We may in this connection refer, for example, to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of other U.S. Constitution, which provides for the, amendment thereof.
RatioOfTheDecision	It will be clearly seen that the power contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of the U.S. Constitution is not ordinary legislative power and no one can possibly call it ordinary legislative power, because the procedure provided for the amendment of the Constitution in Art.
Precedent	V differs radically from the procedure provided for ordinary legislation, for example, the President's assent is not required constitutional amendment under  Art.
Precedent	V of the U.S. Constitution,; Now if Art.
Precedent	368 also had made a similar departure from the procedure provided for ordinary legislation, it could never have (1) [1952 ] 1 S. C. R. 89 said that Art.
Precedent	368 merely contained the procedure for amendment and that what emerges after that procedure is followed is ordinary law of the same quality and nature as emerges after following the procedure for passing ordinary law.
Precedent	If, for example, the assent of the President which is to be found in Art.
Precedent	368 had not been there and the Constitution would have stood amended after the Bill had been passed by the two <ORG> by necessary majority and after ratification by not less than one-half of the States where so required , it could never have been argued that the power to amend the Constitution was contained in Art.
Precedent	245 and 248 read with item 97 of List I and Art.
Precedent	368 merely con- tained the procedure.
RatioOfTheDecision	We are however of opinion that we should look at the quality and nature of what is done under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 and not lay so much str ess on the similarity of the procedure contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 with the procedure for ordinary lawmaking.
RatioOfTheDecision	If we thus look at the quality and nature of what is done under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , we find that it is the exercise of constituent power for the purpose of amending the Constitution itself land is very different from the exercise of ordinary legislative power for passing laws which must be in conformity with the Constitution and cannot go against any provision thereof, unless there is express provision to that effect to which we have already referred.
RatioOfTheDecision	If we thus refer to the nature and quality of what is done under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , we immediately See that what emerges after the procedure in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is gone through is not ordinary law which emerges after the legislative procedure contained in the Constitution is gone through.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thus Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 provides for the coming into existence of what may be called the fundamental law in the form of an amendment of the Constitution and therefore what emerges after the procedure under Art .
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is gone through is not ordinary legislation but an amendment of the Constitution which becoming a part of the fundamental law itself, by virtue of the words contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 to the effect that the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the 'Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged in this connection on behalf of the <ORG> of India that even though the assent of the President is required under Aft.
RatioOfTheDecision	368, the ;President must assent thereto and cannot withhold his assent as is possible in the case of ordinary law in view of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	III of the Constitution, for the words "that he withholds assent therefrom" found in Art.
Precedent	III are not to be found in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	It is however difficult to accept the argument on behalf of the <ORG> that the President cannot withhold his assent when a Bill for amendment of the Constitution is presented to him.
Precedent	Article '368 provides that a Bill for the amendment of the, Constitution shall be presented to the President for his assent.
Precedent	It further provides that upon such assent b y the President, the Constitution shall, stand amended.
Precedent	That in our opinion postulates that if assent is not given, the Constitution cannot be amended.
Precedent	Whether a President will ever withhold his assent in our form of Government is a different matter altogether, but as we road Art.
Precedent	368 we cannot.
Precedent	hold that the President is bound to assent and cannot withhold his assent when a Bill for amendment of the Constitution is presented to him.
Precedent	We are of opinion that 'the President can refuse to give his assent when a Bill for amendment of the Constitution is presented to him, the result being that the Bill altogether falls, for there is no specific provision for anything further to be done,: about the Bill in Art.
Precedent	368 as there is in Art.
Precedent	III.
Precedent	We may in this.
Precedent	connection refer to the different language used in cl.
Precedent	5 of Art.
Precedent	46 of the Irish Constitution which says that "a Bill containing a proposal for the amendment of this Constitution shall be signed by the President Forthwith upon his being satisfied that the provision s of this Article have been complied with, in respect thereof'.
Precedent	It will be seen therefore that if the intention kinder Art.

Precedent	5 of Art.
Precedent	46 of the Irish Constitution.
Precedent	We thus see that in one respect at any rate Art.
Precedent	368 even on its present terms differs from the power of the President in connection with ordinary legislation under the Constitution and that is if the President withholds his assent the Bill for amendment of' the Constitution immediately falls.
RatioOfTheDecision	We cannot accept that the procedure provided under the proviso to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	111 can apply in such a case, for this much cannot be disputed that so far as the procedure for amendment of the Constitution is concerned we must look to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 only and nothing else.
RatioOfTheDecision	In any case the mere fact that the procedure in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is very much assimilated.
RatioOfTheDecision	to the procedure for passing ordinary legislation is no reason for, holding that what emerges after th e procedure under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is followed is ordinary law and no more.
RatioOfTheDecision	We repeat that we must look at the quality and nature of what is done under Art.
Precedent	368 , and that is, the amendment of the Constitution.
Precedent	If we look at that we must bold that what emerges is not ordinary law passed under the Constitution but something which has the effect of amending the fundamental law itself which could not be done by ordinary legislative process under the Constitution unless there is express provision to that effect.
Precedent	We have already referred to such express provisions in various Articles, but Art.
Precedent	368 cannot be treated as such an Article, for it deals specifically with the amendment of the Constitution as a whole.
Precedent	It is also remarkable to note in this connection that the, word "law" which has been used in so many Articles of 'the Consti-  832  tution has been avoided apparently with great care in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	We again refer to the concluding words 368 which says that the "Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the  terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now it is well-known that in the case of ordinary legislation as soon both <ORG> and has received the assent of the main part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	stand amended in ac it is well-known that as the Bill is passed by of the President it becomes an Act.
RatioOfTheDecision	But Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 provides that as soon as the Bill for amendment of the Constitution has been passed in accordance with the procedure provided there in the Constitution shall stand amendmend in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	These words in our opinion have significance of their own.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also remarkable that these words clearly show the difference between the, quality of what emerges after the procedure under Art., 368 is followed and what happens when ordinary law-making procedure is followed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	III, in the case of ordinary law- making when a Bill is passed by the two <ORG> of parliament it is presented to the President and the President shall declare either that he assents to the Bill or that he withholds assent therefrom.
RatioOfTheDecision	But it is remarka ble that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	111 does not provide that when the Bill has been assented to by the President it becomes an Act' The reason for this is that the Bill assented to by the President though it may become law is still not declared by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	I I I to be a law, for such law is open to challenge in courts on various ,grounds, namely, on the ground that it violates any fundamental rights, or on the ground that <ORG> was not competent to pass it or on the ground that it is in breach of any provision of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand we find that when a Bill for the amendment of the Constitution is passed by requisite majority and assented to by the President, the Constitution itself ,declares that the Constitution shall stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	Thereafter what courts can see is whether the procedure provided in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 has been followed, for if that is not done, the Constitution cannot stand amended in accordance with the terms of the Bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	But if the procedure has been followed, t he Constitution stands amended, and there is no question of testing the amendment of the Constitution thereafter on the anvil of fundamental rights or in any other way as in the case of ordinary legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	In view of an this we have no doubt that- even though.
RatioOfTheDecision	by accident the procedure provided in the Constitution for amendment thereof is very akin to the procedure for passing ordinary legislation, the power contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is still not ordinary legislative power but constituent power for the specific purpose of amendment of the Constitution; and it is the quality of that power which determines the nature of what emerges after the procedure in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 has been followed and what thus emerges is not ordinary legislation but fundamental law which cannot be tested,.
RatioOfTheDecision	for example, under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) of the Constitution or under any other provision of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	833  We may briefly refer to an argument on behalf of <ORG> that the amending power contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is same sovereign powe r which was possessed by <ORG> when it made the Constitution and therefore it is not subject to any fetters of any kind.
RatioOfTheDecision	We do not think it necessary to enter into the academic question as to where sovereignty re-sides and whether legal sovereignty is in the people and political.
RatioOfTheDecision	sovereignty in the body which has the power to amend the Constitution and vice versa.
RatioOfTheDecision	In our view the words of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 clearly confer the power to amend the Constitution and also provide the procedure for doing so, and that in our opinion is enough for the purpose of deciding whether the Seventeenth Amendment is valid or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	Further as we have already stated, the power conferred under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is constituent power to change the fundamental law i.e.
RatioOfTheDecision	the Constitution, and is distinct and different from the ordinary legislative power conferred on <ORG> by various other provisions in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	So long as this distinction is kept in mind <ORG> would have the power under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 to amend the Constitu tion and what <ORG> does under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is not ordinary law-making which is subject to Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13 (2) or any other Article of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	What is the extent of the power conferred on <ORG> and whether there are any limitations on it--express or implied-will be considered by us presently.
RatioOfTheDecision	But we have no doubt, without entering into the question of sovereignty and of whether Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 confers the same sovereign power on <ORG> as <ORG> had when framing the Constitution, that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 does confer power on <ORG> subject to the procedure provided therein for amendment of any provision of-the Constitution..
RatioOfTheDecision	This brings us to the scope and extent of the power conferred, for amendment under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 only gives power to amend the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Recourse is had on behalf of the petitioners to the dictionary meaning of the word, "amendment".
RatioOfTheDecision	It is said that amendment implies and means improvement in detail and cannot take in any change in the basic featur es of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Reference in this connection may be made to- the following meaning of the word "'amend" in <ORG>, namely, "to make professed improvements in a, measure before <ORG>; formally, to after in detail, though practically it may be to alter its principle, so as to thwart ".
RatioOfTheDecision	This meaning lit any rate does not support the case of the petitioners that amendment merely means such change as results in improvement in detail.
RatioOfTheDecision	It shows that in-law though amendment MAY professedly, be intended to make- improvements and to alter only in detail, in reality, it may make a radical change in the provision which is amended.
Precedent	In any case, as was pointed out in <NAME> case(1) the word "amend" or "amendment"' is well under-  (1) [1965] 1 S.C.R.
Precedent	933.
Precedent	834  stood in law and will certainly include any change whether by way of addition or alteration or deletion of any provision in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is no reason to suppose that when the word.
RatioOfTheDecision	"amendment" of the Constitution  was being used in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , the intention was to give any meaning less than what we have stated above.
RatioOfTheDecision	To say that "amendment" in law only means a change 'which results in improvement would make amendments impossible, for what- is improvement of an existing law is a matter of opinion and what, for example, the legislature may consider an improvement may not be so considered by others.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is therefore in our opinion impossible to introduce in the concept of amendment as used in Art.
Precedent	368 any idea of improvement as to details of the Constitution.
Precedent	The word "amendment" used in Art.
Precedent	368 must therefore be given its full meaning as used in law and that .means that by amendment an existing Constitution or law can be changed and this change can take the form either of addition to the existing provisions, or alteration of existing provisions and their substitution by others or deletion of certain provisions.
RatioOfTheDecision	altogether.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this connection reference has been made-to contrast certain other provisions of the Constitution,  where, for example the word "amend" has been followed by such words as "by way of addition, variance or repeal" (see Sixth Schedule, paragraph 2-1) and more or less similar expressions in other Articles,of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	it is very difficult to say fact, that no such words appear in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	make any difference, for the meaning of the word why this was done.
RatioOfTheDecision	But the 368 does not in our, mind "amendmend" in law is clearly as indicated above by us and the presence or sense, of explanatory words of the nature indicated above do not in our opinion make any difference.
RatioOfTheDecision	The question whether the power of amendment given by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 also 'includes the power to abrogate the Constitution completely and to replace it by an entire new Constitution, does not really arise in the present case, for the Seventeenth Amendment has not done any such thing and need not be considered.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is enough to say that it may be open to doubt whether the power of amendment contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	568 goes to the extent of completely abrogating th e present Constitution and substituting it by an entirely new one.
RatioOfTheDecision	But short of that, we are of opinion that the power to amend includes the power to add any provision to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	to alter any provision and substitute any other provision in its place and to delete any provision.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Seventeenth Amendment is merely in exercise of the power of amendment a indicated above and cannot be struck down on the ground that it goes beyond the power conferred on <ORG> to amend the Constitution by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	The next question that arises is whether there is any limi- tation on the power of amendment as explained by us above.
RatioOfTheDecision	835  Limitations may be of two kinds, namely, express or implied.
Precedent	So far as express limitations are concerned, there are none such in' Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	When it speaks of the "amendment of this Constitution it obviously and clearly refers to amendment of any provision thereof, including the provisions contained in Part III relating to fundamental rights.
Precedent	Whether Art.
Precedent	13(2) is an express limitati on on the power of amendment will be considered by us law, but so far as, Art.
Precedent	368 is concerned there are no limitation whatsoever in the matter of substance on the amending power and any provision of the Constitution, be it in Part III and any other Part, can be amended under Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	The next question is whether there are any implied limita- tions on the power of amendment contained in Art.
Precedent	368 , and this Wags us to the argument that there are certain basic features of the Constitution which cannot be amended at all and there is an implied limitation on the power of amendment contained in Art.
Precedent	5-68 so far as these basic features are concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	We may in this connection refer to the view prevailing amongst jurists in the United States of America as to whether there are any plied limitations on the power of amendment contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of the U.S. Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	There are two lines of thought in this matter in the United States.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some jurists take the, view that there are certain implied limitations on the  power to amend contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of the U.S. Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	These are said to be with respect to certain basic features, like, the republican character of Government, the federal structure etc.
RatioOfTheDecision	On the other hand, it is that the more prevalent view amongst jurists in the United States is that there are no implied limitations on the scope of the amending power in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of the U.S. Constitution.
Precedent	<NAME> on the Constitutional Law of the United States of America (1936-Edition says that probably the correct position is that the amending power embraces everything; in other words there are no legal limitations whatever on the power of amendment, except what is expressly provided, in Art.
Precedent	V : (see- discussion on pp.
Precedent	1.22 to 127).
Precedent	Even with respect to these express limitations, <NAME> in The Government of the United States (Fifth Edition) at p. 77 says that even these express limitations can be removed and one of the ways of doing so is "to remove, the exception by a preliminary amendment and thus clear the way for fur ther action".
RatioOfTheDecision	Besides, as a matter of fact there is no decision of <ORG> holding that there are implied limitations on the power of amendment contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	V of the U.S. Constitution and all amendments so far made in the United States have been upheld by <ORG> there in the few cases that have been taken to-it for testing the validity of the amendments.
RatioOfTheDecision	836  We have given careful consideration to the argument that certain basic features of our Constitution cannot be amended under Art.
Precedent	368 and have come to, the conclusion that no limitations can be and should be implied upon the power of amendment under Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	One.
RatioOfTheDecision	reason for coming to this conclusion is that if we were to accept that certain basic features of the Constitution cannot be amended under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , it will lead to the position that any amendment made to any Article of the Constitution would be liable to challenge before courts on the ground that it amounts to amendment of a basic feature.
RatioOfTheDecision	<ORG> ent would thus never be able to know what amendments it can make in the Constitution and what it cannot; for, till a complete catalogue of basic features of the Constitution is available, it would be impossible to make any amendment under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 with any certainty that it would be upheld by courts.
RatioOfTheDecision	If such an implied limitation were to be put on the power of amendment contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , it would only be the courts which would have the power to decide what are basic features of the Constitution and then to declare whether a particular amendment is valid or not on the ground that it amends a particular basic feature or not.
RatioOfTheDecision	The .result would be that every amendment made in the Constitution would provide a harvest of legal wrangles so much so that <ORG> may never know what provisions can be amended and what cannot.
RatioOfTheDecision	The power to amend being a constituent power cannot in our opinion for these reasons be held subject to any implied limitations thereon on the ground that certain basic features of the Con stitution cannot be amended.
RatioOfTheDecision	We fail to see why if there was any intention to make any part of the Constitution unamendable, <ORG> failed to indicate it expressly in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	If, for example, the Constitution-makers intended certain provisions in the Constitution, and Part III in particular, to be not amendable, we can see no reason why it was not so stated in Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	On the clear words of Art.
Precedent	368 which provides for amendment of the Constitution which means any provision thereof,.
RatioOfTheDecision	we cannot infer an implied limitation on the power of amendment of any provision of the Constitution', be it basic or otherwise.
RatioOfTheDecision	Our conclusion is that constituent power, like that contained,in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , can only be subject to express limitations and not to any implied limitations so,far as substance of the amendments are concerned and in the absence of anything in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 making any provision of the Constitution unamendable, it Must be held that the power to.
RatioOfTheDecision	amend in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	.3 68 reaches every provision of t he Constitution and can be used to amend any provision thereof provided the procedure indicated, in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 is followed.
RatioOfTheDecision	Copious references were made during the course of arguments to debates in <ORG> and it is urged that it is open to this <ORG> to look into the debates in order to interpret Art.

Precedent	226.
Precedent	The question therefore is whether the Seventeenth Amendment makes any change in Art.
Precedent	226 and whether this change has to be a direct change in the words of Art.

Precedent	226.
Precedent	We are of opinion that when the proviso lays down that there must be ratification when there is any change in the entrenched provisions, including Art.
Precedent	226 , it means that there must be actual change in the terms of the provision concerned.
Precedent	If there is no actual change directly in the entrenched provision, no ratification is required, even if any  amendment of any other provision of the Constitution may have some effect indirectly on the entrenched provisions mentioned in the proviso.
Precedent	But it is urged that there may be such a change in some other provision as would seriously affect an entrenched provision, and in such a case ratification should be necessary.
Precedent	This argument was also dealt with 'in the majority judgment in <NAME> case(2) where the doctrine of pith and substance was applied and it was held that where the amendment in any other Article so affects the entrenched Article as to amount to an amendment therein, then ratification may be necessary, even though the entrenched Article may not be directly touched.
Precedent	Perhaps the use of the doctrine of pith and substance (1)  S. C. R. 89.
Precedent	(2) [1965] 1 S.C.P.
Precedent	933.
RatioOfTheDecision	844  in such a case is not quite apt.
RatioOfTheDecision	But what was meant in <NAME> case(1) was that if there is such an amendment of an unentrenched Article that it will directly affect an entrenched Article and necessitate a change therein, t hen recourse must be had to ratification under the proviso.
RatioOfTheDecision	We may illustrate this by two examples.
RulingByPresentCourt	Article 226 lays down inter alia that <ORG> shall have power to issue writs for the enforcement of any of the rights conferred by Part III and for any other purpose.
RulingByPresentCourt	Now assume that Part III is completely deleted by amendment of the Constitution.

RatioOfTheDecision	We have no doubt that if such a contingency ever happens and Part III is completely deleted, <ORG> will amend Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 also and that will necessitate ratification under the proviso.
RatioOfTheDecision	But suppose <ORG> merely deletes Part III and does not make the necessary consequential amendment in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 , it can then be said that deletion of Part III necessitates change in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 also, and therefore in such a case ratification is necessary, even though <ORG> may not have in fact provide d for amendment of Art 226.

RatioOfTheDecision	We now come to what may be called the argument of fear.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is urged that if Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 confers complete power to amend each and every provision of the Constitution as we have held that it does-frightf ul consequences will follow on such an interpretation.
RatioOfTheDecision	If <ORG> is clothed with such a power to amend the Constitution it may proceed to do away with fundamental rights altogether, it may abolish elected legislatures, it may change the present form of Government, it may do away with the federal structure and create a unitary state instead, and so on.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is therefore argued that we should give a limited interpretation to the power of amendment contained in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , as otherwise we shall be giving power to <ORG> to destroy the Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	This argument is really a political argument and cannot be taken into account in interpreting Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 when its meaning to our mind is clear.
RatioOfTheDecision	But as the argument was urged with a good deal of force on behalf of the petitioners and was met with equal force on behalf of the Union and the States, we propose to deal with it briefly.
RatioOfTheDecision	Now, if this argument means that <ORG> may abuse its power of amendment conferred by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , all that need be said in re ply is that mere possibility of abuse cannot result in courts withholding the power if the Constitution grants it.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is well-settled so far as ordinary laws are concerned that mere possibility of abuse will not induce courts to hold that the power is not there, if the law is valid and its terms clearly confer the power.
RatioOfTheDecision	The same principle in our opinion applies to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the Constitution gives a certain power and its terms are clear, there is no reason why that power should be withheld simply because of possibility of abuse.
RatioOfTheDecision	If we may say so, possibility of abuse of any power granted to any authority is always there; and if possibility of abuse is a reason for withholding the power, no power whatever can ever be conferred on any authority, be it "executive, legislative or even judicial.
RatioOfTheDecision	Therefore, the so-called fear of frightful consequences, which has been urged on behalf of the Petitioners (if we hold, as we do, that the power to amend the Constitution is unfettered by any implied limitations ), is no ground for withholding the power, for we have no reason to suppose that <ORG> on whom such power is ,conferred will abuse it.
Precedent	Further even if it abuses the power of constitutional amendment under Art.
Precedent	368 the check in such circumstances is not in courts but is in the people who elect members of <ORG>.
Precedent	The argument for giving a limited meaning to Art.
Precedent	368 because of possibility of abuse must therefore be rejected.
Precedent	The other aspect of this argument of fear is that we should not make the Constitution too flexible so that it may be open to the requisite majority with the requisite ratification to make changes too frequently in the Constitution.
Precedent	It is said that the Constitution is an organic document for the governance of the country and it is expected to endure and give stability to the institution which it provides.
Precedent	That is undoubtedly so and this is.
Precedent	very true of a written federal Constitution.
Precedent	But a perusal of.
Precedent	various Constitutions of the world shows that there are usually provisions for a mendment of the Constitution in the Constitution itself.
Precedent	This power to amend a Constitution may be rigid or flexible in varying degrees.
Precedent	Jurists have felt that where the power to amend the Constitution is made too rigid and the people outgrow a particular Constitution and feel that it should be amended but cannot do so because of the rigidity of the Constitution, they break the Constitution, and this breaking is more often than not by violent revolution.
Precedent	It is admitted by even those writers on the United States Constitution who are of the view that there are certain basic features which cannot be amended and who would thus make the U. S. Constitution even more rigid' than it is; that howsoever rigid the Constitution may be its rigidity will not stop the people from breaking it if they have outgrown it and this breaking is, generally speaking, by violent revolution.
Precedent	So, making our Constitution rigid by putting the interpretation which the petitioners want us to put on it will not stop the frightfulness which i s conjured up before us on behalf of the petitioners.
Precedent	If anything, an interpretation which will make our Constitution rigid in the manner in which the petitioner want the amending power in Art.
Precedent	368 to be interpreted will make a violent revolution, followed by frightfulness of which the petitioners are afraid, a nearer possibility than an interpretation which will make it flexible.
Precedent	It is clear that our Constitution-makers wanted to avoid' making the Constitution too rigid.
Precedent	It is equally clear that they did not want to make an amendment of the Constitution too easy.
Precedent	They preferred an intermediate course which would make,the Constitution flexible and would still not allow it to be amended too easily.
Precedent	That is why Art.
Precedent	368 provides for special majorities of the two <ORG> for the purpose of amendment of the Constitution.
Precedent	Besides it also provides for ratification by more than half the States in case of entrenched Provisions in the proviso.
Precedent	Subject to these limitations, the Constitution has been, made moderately fl exible to allow any change when the people feel that change is necessary.
Precedent	The necessity for special majorities in each <ORG> separately and, the necessity for, ratification by more than half the States in certain cases appear to us to be sufficient safeguards to prevent too easy change in the Constitution without making it too rigid.
Precedent	But it is said that, in the last sixteen Years, a large number of amendments have been made to the constitution and that shows that the power to amend is much too easy and should be restricted by judicial interpretation.
Precedent	Now, judicial interpretation cannot restrict the power on the basis of a political argument.
Precedent	It has to interpret the Constitution and finds it on the basis of well-known,canons of construction,and on the terms of Art.
Precedent	368 in particular.
Precedent	If on those terms it is clear we think it is-that power to amend is subject to no limitations except those to be expressly found in the Constitution, courts must give effect to that.
Precedent	The fact that 'm the last sixteen years a large  number of amendments could be made and have been made is in our opinion due to the accident that one party has been returned by electors in sufficient strength to be able to command the special majorities which are required under Art.
Precedent	368 , not only at the <ORG> but also in all the Stites.
Precedent	It' is because of this circumstance that we have had so many amendments in the course of the last sixteen years.
Precedent	But that in our opinion is no ground for limiting the clear words of Art.
Precedent	368.
Precedent	The power of amendment contained in a written federal Con- stitution is a safety valve which to a large extent provides for stable growth and makes violent revolution more or less unnecessary.
Precedent	It has been said by text-book writers that the power of amendment, though it allows for change, also makes a Constitution long lived and stable and serves the needs of the people from time to time.
Precedent	If this power to amend is made too rigid it loses its value as a safety valve.
Precedent	The more rigid a Constitution the more likely it is that people wil l outgrow it and throw it over-board violently.
Precedent	On the other hand, if the Constitution is flexible (though it may not be made too easy to modify it) the power of amendment provides for stability of the Constitution itself and for ordered progress of the nation.
Precedent	If therefore there had to be a choice between giving an interpretation-to Art.
Precedent	368 which would make our Constitution rigid and giving an interpretation which would make it flexible, we would prefer to make it flexible, so that it may endure for a long period of time and may, if necessary, be amended from time to time in accordance with the progress in the ideas of the people for whom it is meant.
Precedent	But we feel that it is not necessary to go to this extent, for that would be entering into the field of politics.
Precedent	As we see the terms of Art.
Precedent	368 , we are clearly Df opinion that the Constitutionmakers wanted to make our Constitution reasonably flexible and ,.that the interpretation that we have given to Art.
Precedent	368 is in consonance with the terms thereof and the  intention of those who made it.
Precedent	We therefore reject the argument of fear altogether.

Precedent	It is an error to view our Constitution as if it were a mere organisational document by which the people established the atructure and the mechanism of their Government.
Precedent	Our Constitution is intended to be much more because it aims at being a social document In which the relationship of society to the indiVidual and of Government to both and the rights of the minorities and the backward classes are clearly laid down.
Precedent	This social document is headed by a Preamble* which epitomizes the principles on which the Government is intended to function and these principles are later expanded into Fundamental Rights in Part III and the Directive Principles of Policy in Part TV.
