RatioOfTheDecision	The matter, however, has to be looked at in this way.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where the Constitution is overthrown and the <ORG> lose their position under the old Constitution, they m ay not be able to pass on the validity of the new Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is the, result of a revolution pure and simple.
RatioOfTheDecision	Where the new Constitution is not accepted and the people have not acquiesced in the change and the courts under the old Constitution function, the courts can declare the new Constitution to be void.
RatioOfTheDecision	Perhaps even when the people acquiesce and a new Government comes into being, the courts may still declare the new Constitution to be invalid but only if moved to do so.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is only when the courts begin to function under the new Constitution that they cannot consider the vires of that Constitution because then they owe their existence to it.
RatioOfTheDecision	I agree with <NAME> in these observations taken from his book.
RatioOfTheDecision	He, however, does not include amendments of the Constitution in these remarks and expressly omits them.
RatioOfTheDecision	His opinion seems to indicate that in the case of amendments courts are completely free to see that the prescribed constitutional mode, of alteration is complied with and the alteration is withi n the permissive limits to which the Constitution wishes the amendments to go.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is true of all amendments but particularly of an amendment seeking to repeal the courts' decision and being small in dimension, leaves the courts free to consider its validity.
RatioOfTheDecision	The courts derive the power from the existing terms of the Constitution and the amendment fails if it seeks to overbear some existing restriction on legislation.
RatioOfTheDecision	What I have said does not mean that Fundamental Rights are not subject to change or modification.
RatioOfTheDecision	In the most inalienable of such rights a distinction must be made between possession of a right and its exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	The first is fixed and the latter controlled by justice and necessity.
Precedent	Take for example Art.
Precedent	21 :   "No person shall be deprived of his life or personal liberty except according to procedure established by law".
Precedent	Of all the rights, the right to one's life, is the most valuable.
Precedent	This article of the Constitution, therefore, makes.

RatioOfTheDecision	The deprivation, when it takes place, is not of the right which was immutable but of the continued exercise of the right.
RatioOfTheDecision	Take a Directive Principle which is not enforceable at law but where the same result is reached.
RatioOfTheDecision	The right to employ- ment is a directive principle.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some countries even view it as a Fundamental Right.
RatioOfTheDecision	The exercise, however, of that right must depend upon the capacity of Society to afford employment to all and sundry.
RatioOfTheDecision	The possession of this right cannot be confused with its exercise.
RatioOfTheDecision	One right here is positive and can be enforced although its, exercise can be curtailed or taken away, the other is a right which, the <ORG> must try to give but which cannot be enforced.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution permits a curtailment of the exercise of most of the Fundamental Rights by stating the limits of that curtailment.
RatioOfTheDecision	But this power does not permit the, <ORG> itself, to take away or abridge the right beyond the limits set by the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	It must also be remem bered that the rights of one% individual are often opposed by the rights of another individual and thus also become limitative.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution in this way" permits the Fundamental Rights to be controlled in their exercise but prohibits their erasure.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is argued that such approach makes Society static and robs the <ORG> of its sovereignty.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is submitted that it leaves revolution as the holy alternative if change is necessary.
RatioOfTheDecision	This is not right.

Precedent	The question may now be 885, asked,:why- was it necessary to make such a Fundamental Right at all ?
Precedent	There is no natural right in property and as <NAME> said in his Reflections, Government is not made in virtue of natural rights, which may and do exist in total independence of it.
Precedent	Natura l rights embrace activity outside the status of citizen.
Precedent	Legal rights are required for free existence as a social being and the <ORG> undertakes to protect them.
RatioOfTheDecision	Fundamental Rights are those rights which the <ORG> enforces against itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Looking at the matter briefly but historically, it may be said that the Greeks were not aware of these distinctions for as <NAME>) points out they did not distinguish between personality as a citizen and personality as a human being.
RatioOfTheDecision	For them the Individual was merged in the citizen and the citizen in the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	There was personal liberty and private law but there was no sharp division between the different kinds of laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Romans evolved this gradually not when the Roman Republic existed, but when the notion of a Fiscus developed in the Empire And the legal personality of the Individual was separated from his membership of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was then that the <ORG> began to recognize the rights of the Individual in his dealings with the <ORG>.
Precedent	It was Cicero(2) who was the fi rst to declare that the' primary duty of the Governor of a State was to secure to each individual in the possession of his property.
Precedent	Here we may see a recognition of the ownership of property as a Fundamental Right.
Precedent	This idea wasso engrained in early social philosophy that we find <NAME> opining in his Civil Government' (Ch.
Precedent	7) that "Government has no other end but the preservation of property".
Precedent	The concepts of liberty, equality and religious freedom were well-known.
Precedent	To them was added the concept of property rights.

Precedent	The term property in its pristine meaning embraced only land but it soon came to mean much more.
Precedent	According to <NAME>)_ "Property is any protected right or bundle of rights (interest or thing) with direct, or indirect regard to any external object (i.e.
Precedent	other than the person himself) which is material or  quasi material (i.e.
Precedent	a protected ,process) and which the then and there <ORG> permits to be either private or public, which is connoted by the legal concepts of occupying, possessing or, using".

RatioOfTheDecision	The contention that article 368 prescribes only th e procedure of amendment cannot be accepted.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article not only prescribes the procedure but also gives the power of amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the procedure of art.368 is followed, the Constitution "shall stand amended" in accordance with the terms of the bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is because the power to amend is given by the article that the Constitution stands amended.
RatioOfTheDecision	The proviso is enacted on the assumption that the several articles mentioned in it are amendable.
RatioOfTheDecision	The object of the proviso is to lay down a stricter procedure for amendment of the articles which would otherwise have been amendable under the easier procedure of the main part.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no other provision in the Constitution under which these articles can be amended.
RatioOfTheDecision	Articles 4, 169, Fifth Schedule Part D, and Sixth Schedule Para 21 empower the <ORG> to.
RatioOfTheDecision	pass laws amending the provisions of the First, Fourth, Fifth and Sixth Schedules and making amendments of the Constitution consequential on the abolition or creation of the legislative councils in States, and by e xpress provision no such law is deemed to be an amendment of the Constitution for the purposes of art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368.
RatioOfTheDecision	All other provisions of the Constitution can be amended by recourse to art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 only.
RatioOfTheDecision	No other article confers the power of amending the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Some articles are expressed to continue until provision is made by law [see articles 59(3), 65(3), 73(2), 97, 98(3), 106, 135, 142(1), 148(3), 149, 171(2), 186, 187(3), 189(3), 194(3), 195, 221(2), 283(1) and (2), 285, 313, 345, 372(1), 373].
RatioOfTheDecision	Some articles continue unless provision is made otherwise by law [see articles 120(2), 133(3), 210(2) and some continue save as otherwise provided by law [see articles 239(1), 287].
RatioOfTheDecision	Some articles are subject to the provisions of any law to be made [see articles 137, 146(2), 225, 229(2), 241(3), 300(1), 309], and some are expressed not to derogate from the power of making laws [see articles 5 to 11, 289(2)].
RatioOfTheDecision	All these articles are transitory in nature and cease to operate when provision is made by law on the subject.
RatioOfTheDecision	None of them can be regarded as conferring the power of 'amendment of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Most of articles continue until provision is made by law made by the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	But some of them continue until or unless provision is made by <ORG> (see articles 189 (3), 194 (3), 195, 210(2), 229(2), 300(1), 345) or by the appropriate legislature (see articles 225, 241(3)); these articles do not confer a power of amendment, for the State legislature cannot amend the Constitution.

Precedent	In <NAME> v. Madison(1), <NAME>, C.J., said:  "It is a proposition too plain to be contested, that the Constitution controls any legislative Act repugnant to it; or, that the legislature may alter the Constitution by an ordinary Act.
Precedent	Between these alternatives there is no middle ground.
Precedent	The Constitution is either a superior paramount law, unchangeable by ordinary means, or it is on a level with (1) [1803] 1 Cranch 137,177:.
Precedent	2 L. Ed.
Precedent	60,  73.
Precedent	Ordinary legislative Acts, and, like other Acts, is alter- able when the legislature shall please to alter it.
RatioOfTheDecision	If the former part of the alternative be  true, then a legislative Act contrary to the Constitution is not law; if the, latter part be true, then written constitutions are absurd attempts, on the part of the people, to limit a power in its own nature illimitable.
RatioOfTheDecision	Certainly all those who have framed written constitutions contemplate them as forming the fundamental and paramount law of the nation, and, consequently, the theory of every such government must be, that an Act of the Legislature, repugnant to the Constitution, is void.
RatioOfTheDecision	This theory is essentially attached to a written constitution, and is consequently to be considered, by this court, as one of the fundamental principles of our society."
RatioOfTheDecision	It is because a Constitution Amendment Act can amend the Constitution and is not a law that art.

RatioOfTheDecision	368 is very different from the ordinary legislative proms.

RatioOfTheDecision	The bill must be passed in each <ORG> by not less than two thirds of the members present and voting, the requisite quorum in each <ORG> being a majority of its total membership; and in cases coming under the proviso, the amendment must be ratified by the legislature of not less than one half of the States.
RulingByPresentCourt	Upon the bill so passed being assented to by the President, the Constitution stands.
RulingByPresentCourt	amended in accordance with the  terms of the bill.
RatioOfTheDecision	The ordinary legislative process is much easier.
RatioOfTheDecision	A bill initiating a law may be passed by a majority of the members present and voting at a sitting of each <ORG> or at a joint sitting of the <ORG>s, the quorum for the meeting of either <ORG> being one tenth of the total number of members of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The bill so passed on being assented to by the President becomes a law.
RatioOfTheDecision	A bill though passed by all the members of both <ORG>s cannot take effect as a Constitution amendment Act unless it is initiated for the express purpose of amending the Constitution.

Precedent	1 , P. 20:  .lm15 "The term 'constitution' is ordinarily employed to designate the organic law in contradistinction to the term law, which is generally used to designate statutes Or legislative enactments.

Precedent	Demands for safeguards of the rights embodied in Part III and IV may be traced to the Constitution of India Bill 1895, the Congress Resolutions between 1917 and 1919, Mrs. <NAME>'s Commonwealth of India Bill of 1925, the Report of the Nehru Committee set up under the Congress Resolution in 1927, the Congress Resolution of March.
RatioOfTheDecision	1931 and the Sapru Report of 1945.
RatioOfTheDecision	The American bill of rights,the constitutions of other countries the declaration of human rights by <ORG> and other declarations and charters gave impetus to the demand.
RatioOfTheDecision	In this background <ORG> embodied in preamble to the Constitution the resolution to secure to all citizens social, economic and political justic e, liberty of thought, expression, belief, faith and worship, equality of status and opportunity and fraternity assuring the dignity of the individual and the unity of the nation and incorporated safeguards as to some human rights in Parts III and IV of the Constitution after separating them into two parts on the Irish model.
RatioOfTheDecision	Part III contains the passive obligations of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	It enshrines the right of life, personal liberty, expression, assembly, movement, residence, avocation, property, culture and education, constitutional remedies, and protection against exploitation and obnoxious penal laws.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> shall not deny these rights save as provided in the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Part IV contains the active obligations of the <ORG>.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> shall secure a social order in which social, economic and political justice shall inform all the institutions of national life.
RatioOfTheDecision	Wealth and its source of production shall not be concentrated in the hands of the few but shall be distributed so as to subserve the common good, an d there shall be adequate means of livelihood for all and equal pay for equal work.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> shall endeavour to secure the health and strength of workers, the right to work, to education and to assistance in cases of want, just and humane conditions of work, a living wage for workers, a uniform civil code, free and compulsory education for children.
RatioOfTheDecision	The <ORG> shall take steps to organize village panchayats, promote the educational and economic interests of the weaker sections of the people, raise the level of nutrition and standard of living, improve public health.
RatioOfTheDecision	organize agricultural and animal husbandry separate the judiciary from executive and promote international peace and security.
RatioOfTheDecision	The active obligations of the <ORG> under Part IV are not justiciable.
RatioOfTheDecision	If a law made by the <ORG> in accordance with the fundamental directives of Part IV comes in conflict with the fundamental rights embodied in Part II the law to the extent of repugnancy is void.

RatioOfTheDecision	Instead of amend- ing each article separately, the <ORG> has by art.

RatioOfTheDecision	The Act s as they stood on the date of the Constitution Amendments are validated.
RatioOfTheDecision	By the last part of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31B the competent legislatures will continue to the power to repeal or amend the Acts.
RatioOfTheDecision	The subsequent repeals and amendments are not validated.
RatioOfTheDecision	If in future the competent legislature passes a repealing or amending Act which is inconsistent with Part III it will be void.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have, therefore, coma to the conclusion that the First, Fourth, Sixteenth and Seventeenth Amendments are constitutional and am not void.
RatioOfTheDecision	If so, it is common ground that these petitions must be For the last 16 years the validity of constitutional amendments of fundamental rights have been recognized by the people and all the organs of the government 'including the  legislature, the judiciary and the executive.
Fact	Revolutionary, social and economic changes have taken place on the strength of the First, Fourth and Seventeenth Amendments.

RatioOfTheDecision	Having heard lengthy arguments on the question I have come to the conclusion that the validity of the constitutional amendments was rightly upheld in Sri Sankari <NAME>) and <NAME>) cases and I find no reason for over-ruling them.

RatioOfTheDecision	What has been done cannot be undone.
RatioOfTheDecision	The battle for the past is lost.

RatioOfTheDecision	To say that they were valid in the past and will be invalid in the future is to amend the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	Such a naked power of amendment of the Constitution is not given to the Judges.
RatioOfTheDecision	The argument for the petitioners suffers from a double fallacy, the first that the <ORG> has no power to amend Part III so as to abridge or take away the entrenched property rights, and the second that the Judges have the power to make such an amend- ment.
RatioOfTheDecision	I may  add that if the First and the Fourth amendments are valid, the Seventeenth must necessarily be valid.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is not possible to say that the First and Fourth amendments though originally invalid have now been validated by acquiescence.

Precedent	The provisions of the Constitution relating to the amendment of the Constitution divide the Articles of the Constitution into two groups.

RatioOfTheDecision	It is necessary at this stage to set out briefly the history of Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	..31-A and 31-B.

Precedent	Soon after the promulgation of the Constitution, the political party in power, commanding as it did a majority of votes in the several State legislatures as well as in <ORG>, carried out radical measures of agrarian reform in Bihar, may be referred to as Zamindari Abolition Acts.
Precedent	Certain zamindars, feeling themselves aggrieved, attacked the validity of those Acts in courts of law on the ground that they contravened the 'fundamental rights conferred on them by Part III of the Constitution.
Precedent	<ORG> held that the Act passed iii Bihar was unconstitutional while <ORG> of Allahabad and Nagpur upheld the validity of the corresponding legislation in Uttar Pradesh and Madhya Pradesh respectively (See Kameshwar Uttar Pradesh and Madhya Pradesh by enacting legislation which v. State of Bihar(1) and <CITATION_629631> (1).
Precedent	The parties aggrieved by these respective decisions had filed appeals by special  leave before this <ORG>.At the same time petitions had also been preferred before this <ORG> under Art.
Precedent	32 by certain other Zamindars, seeking the determination of the same issues It was atstage that <ORG>, with a view to put an endall this litigation and to remedy what they considered to be certain defects brought to light in the work of the Constitution, brought forward a bill to amend the Constitution, which,.
Precedent	after undergoing amendments in various particulars, was passed by the require majority as the Constitution (First Amendment) Act, 1951 by which Arts.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A and 31-B were added to the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	That was the first step taken by <ORG> to assist the process of legislation to bring about agrarian reform, by introducing Articles 31-A and 31- B.
RatioOfTheDecision	The second step in the same direction was taken by <ORG> in 1955 by amending Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	31-A by the Constitution (Fourth Amendment) Act, 1955..
RatioOfTheDecision	The object of this amendment was to widen the scope of agrarian reform and to confer on the legisla tive measures adopted in that behalf immunity from a possible attack that they contravened' the fundamental rights of citizens.
RatioOfTheDecision	In other words, the amendment Protected the legislative measures in respect of certain other items of agrarian and social welfare legislation, which affected the proprietary rights of certain citizens.
Precedent	At the time when the first amendment was made, Art.
Statute	31-B expressly provided that none, of the, Acts and Regulations specified in the 9th Schedule, nor any of the provisions thereof, shall be deemed to be void or ever to have become void on the ground that they were inconsistent with or took: away or abridged any of the rights conferred by Part III, and it added that notwithstanding any judgment, decree or order of any <ORG> or tribunal to the contrary, each of the said Acts and Regulations shall subject to the power of any competent legislature to repeal or amend, continue in force.
Statute	At this time, 19 Acts were listed in Schedule 9, and they were thus effectively validated.
Statute	One more Act  was added to this list by the Amendment Act of 1955, so that as a result of the second amendment, the Schedule contained 20 Acts which were validated.
RatioOfTheDecision	It appears that notwithstanding these amendments, certain other legislative measures adopted by different States for the purpose of giving effect to the agrarian policy of the party in power, were effectively challenged.

Argument	The first question presented for determination in this case is whether the impugned Act, in so far a s it purports to take away or abridge any of the fundamental rights conferred by Part III .or the Constitution,falls within the prohibition of Art.
Argument	13 (2) which provides that "the <ORG>, 'shall, not make any law which takes away or abridges the rights conferred by this Part and any law made in contravention of this clause shall to the extent of the Contravention, be void".
Argument	In other words, the argument, of the petitioners was that the law to which Art, 13(2) applies, would include a law passed by <ORG> by virtue of its constituent power to amend the Constitution, and so, its validity will have.
RatioOfTheDecision	to be tested by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was contended that the <ORG>" includes <ORG> within Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	12 and "law" must include, a constitutional amendment.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was said that it was the deliberate intention of the framers of the Constitution, who realised the sanctity of the fundamental rights conferred by Part III, to make them immune from interference not only by ordinary laws passed by the legislatures in the count ry but also from constitutional amendments.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, there is no substance in this argument.- Although "law" must ordinarily include constitutional law, there is 'a juristic distinction between ordinary law made in exercise of legislative power and constitutional law which is Made in exercise of constituent power.
RatioOfTheDecision	In a written federal form of Constitution there is a clear and well-known distinction between the law of the Constitution and ordinary law made by the legislature on the basis of separation of powers and (1)7 S.C.R.82.
Precedent	931  pursuant to the power of law-making conferred by the Consti- tution (See <NAME> on 'Law of the Constitution, Tenth: Edn.
Precedent	p. 110, <NAME>, 'Law and the Constitution' pp.
Precedent	62-64, and 'American Jurisprudence', 2nd Edn.
Precedent	Vol.
Precedent	16, p. 181).
Precedent	In such a written Constitution, the amendment of the Constitution is .a substantive, constituent act which is made in the exercise, of the sovereign power which created the Constitution and which is effected by a special means, namely, by a pre designed fundamental procedure unconnected with ordinary legislation.
Precedent	The amending power under Art.
Precedent	368 is hence sui generis and cannot be, compared to the law- making power of <ORG> pursuant to Art.
Precedent	246 read with List I and 111.
Precedent	It follows that the expresSion "law" in Art.
Precedent	13(2) of the Constitution cannot be construed as including an amendment of the Constitution which is by <ORG> in exercise of, its sovereign constituent power, but must mean law made by <ORG> in its legislative capacity :pursuant to the powers of law-making given by the Constitution itself under Art.
Precedent	246 read with Lists I and In of the 7th Schedule.
Precedent	It is also clear, on the same line of reasoning, that 'law' in Art.
Precedent	13(2) cannot be construed so as to include 'law' made by <ORG> under Arts.
Precedent	4, 169, 392, 5th Schedule Part D and 6th Schedule para 2 1.
RatioOfTheDecision	The amending power of <ORG> exercised under these Articles stands on the same as the constitutional amendment made under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	U8 so far as Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	13(2) is concerned and doe s not fall within the definition of law within the meaning of this last article.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is necessary to add that the definition of 'law' in Art.
Precedent	13(3) does not include in terms a constitutional amendments though it includes "any Ordinance,, order, bye-law, rule, regulation, notification, custom or usage ".
Precedent	It should be noticed that The language.
Precedent	of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	3 6 8 is perfectly general and empowers <ORG> to amend the Constitution without any exception Whatsoever.
RatioOfTheDecision	H I ad it been intended by the Constitution-makers that the fundamental rights guaranteed under Part III should be completely outside the scope of Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 , it is reasonable to assume that they would have made an express provision to that effect.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was stressed by the petitioners during the course 'of the, argument that Part III is headed as 'Fundamental Rights" and that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	32 " guarantee's' the right to move <ORG> by appropriate proceedings for enforcement of rights conferred by Part M. But the expression "fundamental" in the phrase "Fun damental Rights" means that such rights are fundamental vis-a-vis the laws of the legislatures and the acts of the executive authorities mentioned in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	12.
RatioOfTheDecision	It cannot be suggested, that the expression "fundamental" lifts the fundamental rights above the Constitution itself.
RatioOfTheDecision	Similarly, the expression "guaranteed' in Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	32(1) and 32(4) means that the right to move <ORG> for enforcement of fundamental rights without exhausting the, normal channels through <ORG> or the lower courts is guaranteed.
RatioOfTheDecision	This expression also does not place the fundamental rights above the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	I proceed to consider the next question arising in this case, the scope of the amending power under Art.
RulingByLowerCourt	368 of the Constitution.
RulingByLowerCourt	It is contended on behalf of the petitioners that Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	368 merely lays down the procedure for amendment and does not vest the amending power as such in any agency constituted under that article.
RatioOfTheDecision	I am unable to accept this argument as correct Part XX of the Constitution which contains on ly Art.
Precedent	368 is described as a Part dealing with the Amendment of the Constitution and Art.

RatioOfTheDecision	As I have already said, the effect of the impugned Act on the jurisdiction of <ORG> under Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	226 of the, Con- stitution is not direct but only incidental in character and therefore the contention " of the petitioners on this point against the validity of the impugned Act must be rejected.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is well-settled that in examining a constitutional question of this character, it is legitimate to consider whether the impugned legislation is a legislation directly in respect of the subject-matter covered by any particular article of the Constitution or whether touches the said article s only incidentally or indirectly.
RatioOfTheDecision	<CITATION_1857950> (4), kania , C.J., had occasion to consider the validity of the argument that, the Preventive detention order resulted in the detention of the applicant in a cell, and so, it contravened his fundamental rights guaranteed by (1) 258 U. S. 130 .

RatioOfTheDecision	In spite of the saving clauses (2) to (6), permitting abridgement of the rights connected with each of them, punitive detention under several sections of the Penal Code , i.e., for theft, cheating, forgery and even ordinary assault, will be illegaL Unless such conclusion necessarily follows from the article, it is obvious that such construction should be avoided.
RatioOfTheDecision	In my opinion, suc h result is.
RatioOfTheDecision	clearly not the outcome of the Constitution.
RatioOfTheDecision	The article has to be read without any pre-conceived notions.
RatioOfTheDecision	So read, it clearly means that the legislation to be examined must be directly in respect of one of the rights mentioned in the sub-clauses.
RatioOfTheDecision	If there is a legislation directly attempting to control a citizen's freedom of speech or ex- pression, or his right to assemble peaceably and without arms, etc., the question whether that legislation is saved by the relevant saving clause of article 19 will arise.
RatioOfTheDecision	If, however, the legislation is not directly in respect of any of these subjects but as a, result of the operation of other legislation, for instance, for punitive or preventive detention, his right under any of these sub- clauses is abridged, the question of the application of article 19 does not arise.
RatioOfTheDecision	'Me true approach is only to consider the direct- ness of the legislation and not what will be the result of the detention otherwise valid, on the mode of the detenu's life .
RatioOfTheDecision	On that , short ground, in my opinion, this argument about the infringement of the rights mentioned in article 19(1) generally must fail.

RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that the principle of stare decisis may not strictly apply to, a decision on a constitutional point.
RatioOfTheDecision	There is no restriction in the Constitution itself which prevents this Court from reviewing its earlier decisions or even to depart from them in the interest of public good.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is true that the problem of construing constitutional provisions cannot be adequately solved by merely adopting the literal construction of the words used in, the various articles.
RatioOfTheDecision	The Constitution is  an organic document and it is intended to serve as a guide to the solution of changing problems which the <ORG>' may have to face from time to time.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is manifest that in a progressive and dynamic society the character of these problems is bound to change with the inevitable consequence that the relevant words used in the Constitution may also change their meaning and significance.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even so., the <ORG> is reluctant to accede to, the suggestion that its earlier decisions should be frequently reviewed or departed from.
RatioOfTheDecision	In such a case the test should be : what is the nature of the error alleged in the earlier decision, what is its impact on the public good and what is the compelling character of the considerations urged in support of the contrary view.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also a relevant factor that the earlier decision has been followed in, a large number of cases, that titles to property have passed and multitude of rights and obligations have been created in consequence of the earlier decision.
RatioOfTheDecision	I have already dealt with t he merits of the contention of the petitioners with regard to the validity of the impugned Act and I have given reasons for holding that the impugned Act is constitutionally valid and the contentions ,of the petitioners are unsound.
RatioOfTheDecision	Even on the assumption that it is possible to take a different view and to hold that the impugned Act is unconstitutional I am of opinion that the principle of state decisis must be applied to the present case and the plea made by the, petitioners for reconsideration of Sankari Prasad(1) case and the decision in <CITATION_1308308> (2) is wholly unjustified and must be rejected.
Precedent	In Writ Petition No.
Precedent	202 of 1966, it was contended by Mr. <NAME> that the continuance of the Proclamation of Emergency under Art.
Precedent	352 of the Constitution was a gross violation of power because the emergency had ceased to exist.
Precedent	It was also contended that Art.
Precedent	358 should be so construed as to confine its operation on to legislative or executive action relevant to the Proclamation of Emergency.
RatioOfTheDecision	It was submi tted that the Mysore State was rot a border area and the land reform legislation of that State had no relevant-connection with the Proclamation of Emergency and the fundamental rights conferred by Art.
RatioOfTheDecision	19 cannot be suspended so far as the petitions are concerned.
RatioOfTheDecision	I do not think that it is necessary to express any opinion on these points because the Writ Petition must fail on the other grounds which I have already discussed above.
RatioOfTheDecision	It is also not necessary for me to express an opinion on the doctrine of prospective overruling of legislation.
RulingByPresentCourt	For the reasons already expressed I hold that all these petitions fail and should be dismissed, but there will be no order as to Petitions dismissed.
